An-Nazzam's Denial of Quranic Inimitability and the Theory of Sarfah
An-Nazzam's denial of Qur'anic inimitability and his theory of sarfah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an-Nazzam's argument that the Qur'an is not miraculously composed and his theory of sarfah, and why this view is rejected.

Core Idea
An-Nazzam argued the Qur'an's miracle is not its composition but God turning people away from challenging it.

This lesson presents the controversial claim of an-Nazzam, a Mu'tazili scholar, who denied the intrinsic inimitability of the Qur'an. He argued that since no single verse is by itself inimitable, the whole cannot be miraculous, and he proposed instead the theory of 'sarfah'—a divine intervention that prevented people from opposing the Qur'an despite its being within their power. Understanding this claim is essential to grasp why mainstream scholars emphasize the Qur'an's own linguistic miracle.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp an-Nazzam's overall argument. Then read the translation to ensure understanding.
An-Nazzam's argument is based on a logical extension from individual to whole.
2
Identify his main premises
An-Nazzam argues that no single phrase like 'al-Hamdu lillah' is miraculously inimitable, so the whole cannot be. Notice how he generalizes from parts to the whole.
He uses a similar reasoning for tawatur and ijma.
3
Understand the sarfah theory
Learn what sarfah means: God turned people away from challenging the Qur'an despite it being possible for them. He gives the beard example to illustrate that sarfah itself can be miraculous.
This is his alternative explanation for the Qur'an's miracle.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this theory undermines the Qur'an's own claim to be a literary miracle. How does it affect the proof of prophethood?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعم النظام أن القرآن ليس بمعجوز النظم وأن كل معجم متبلد يقدر أن يأتي بمثل نظمه العجيب ووصفه البديع، واحتجاجه لذلك أنه متى يعجز الإنسان أعند قوله: الحمد لله أم عند قوله: رب العالمين أم عند قوله: الرحمن الرحيم، وادعاء العجز عند كل من ذلك باطل لما أن ما دون سورة ليس بمعجز عندكم، ثم إذا لم يكن أفراد الآيات معجوزاً عنها، فكذا المجموع منها إذ حكم الجمل لا يخالف حكم الأفراد، ولهذا زعم أن المتواتر من الأخبار محتمل للكذب لأن خبر كل فرد من أهل التواتر محتمل للكذب فكذا عند الاجتماع، وأن الخطأ على الإجماع جائز لجوازه على كل فرد من المجتمعين، وإنما الإعجاز حصل بالصرفة ومعناها: أنه تعالى صرف همتهم بلطفه عن الاشتغال بمعارضته أنه ممكن، ومثل هذا مع معجز لما فيه من نقض العادة حتى إن من جعل دليل رسالته أن يقبض على لحية وهم لا يقبضون على ذلك، ثم إنه قبض على لحيته وتحدى الناس إلى ذلك فلم يقبض واحد منهم على لحيته كان ذلك معجزاً لما فيه من صرف هممهم عن الأمر الممكن لا أن ذلك ليس في مقدور في نفسه. هذا منه كلام باطل واحتجاج فاسد، فإن أقرب ما يجاب عنه أن يقال: ائت بقصيدة مثل قصيدة امرئ القيس: قفا نبك، أو مثل قصيدته: ألا أنعم صباحًا أيها الطلل البالي، أو مثل قصيدته خليلي مرا بي على أم جندب أو مثل قصيدته: شمالك شرق بعدما كان أقصر، وغيرها من القصائد له ولغيره من فحول الشعراء كالنابغة وطرفه وزهير والأعشى ليظهر عند عجزه عن ذلك أنه مكابر فيما يقول، ويقال له : متى عجزت أنت أعند التكلم بالكلمة الأولى أو عند التكلم بالكلمة [؟]
And what an-Nazzam claims that the Qur'an is not miraculous in composition and that every mute idiot is able to produce similar to its wondrous composition and beautiful description. His proof for that is: when does man become incapable? At His saying 'Al-Hamdu lillah'? Or at His saying 'Rabb al-'Alamin'? Or at His saying 'Ar-Rahman ar-Rahim'? The claim of incapacity at each of that is false because what is less than a sura is not miraculous according to you. Then if individual verses are not incapable of being matched, then likewise the whole of them, because the ruling of the whole does not differ from the ruling of the individuals. For this reason he claimed that the mutawatir reports are possible of lying because the report of each individual of the people of tawatur is possible of lying, so likewise when they come together; and that error in consensus is permissible because it is permissible for each individual of the gatherers. And that the miracle occurred only by the sarfah, and its meaning is that God Most High turned away their resolve by His subtlety from engaging in opposing it, it being possible. And something like this is miraculous because it involves the breaking of custom, so much so that if one makes the evidence of his message that he should seize a beard and they do not seize that, then he seized his beard and challenged people to that, and not one of them seized his beard, that would be miraculous because it involves turning their resolve away from a possible action, not that it is not in power in itself. This from him is false speech and corrupt argument. The nearest answer to it is to say: Bring a poem like the poem of Imru' al-Qais: 'Qifa nabki' or like his poem 'Ala an'am sabahan ayyuha at-talal al-bali' or like his poem 'Khalili murra bi 'ala Ummi Jundub' or like his poem 'Shimaluka sharq ba'da ma kana aqsar' and other poems of his and of other leading poets like an-Nabigha, Tarafa, Zuhayr, and al-A'sha, so that when you are incapable of that it becomes apparent that you are obstinate in what you say, and it is said to him: When are you incapable? At the utterance of the first word or at the utterance of the word [؟]
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the claim of an-Nazzam (a Mu'tazili) regarding the Qur'an's inimitability. He denied that the composition of the Qur'an is miraculous, arguing that no single phrase like 'al-Hamdu lillah' is individually miraculous, therefore the whole cannot be miraculous. He extended this logic to claim that mutawatir reports are not certain (since individual reports can lie) and that consensus can err. He held that the only miracle is the 'sarfah'—God turning people's resolve away from challenging the Qur'an, despite it being possible. The author refutes this by calling it false and corrupt. The refutation employs a counter-challenge: if an-Nazzam claims he or anyone can produce similar, let him produce a poem comparable to the famous odes of Imru' al-Qais and other master poets. His inability will show his obstinacy. The passage then repeats the same logic: just as an-Nazzam asked 'when are you incapable?' regarding the Qur'an, he is asked the same regarding poetry: is his incapacity at the first word or the next? The text cuts off, but the argument is clear: the challenge demonstrates that even non-revealed literary masterpieces are recognized as beyond human power to imitate, so the Qur'an is even more so.

Key Terms
النظام
الصرفة
إعجاز القرآن
المعارضة
التواتر
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'an's miracle is that God prevented people from challenging it (sarfah), not that its composition is inherently miraculous.
Correction: Mainstream Sunni scholars hold that the Qur'an's composition is itself the primary miracle, and sarfah is not the accepted explanation.

Checkpoint
What did an-Nazzam claim was the real miracle of the Qur'an instead of its composition?
A) Its eloquence
B) The sarfah (turning away of people)
C) Its prophecies
D) Its legal rulings

Key Takeaway
An-Nazzam's theory of sarfah denies the inherent miraculous nature of the Qur'anic text, a view rejected by Sunni scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
An-Nazzam argued that if each part of the Qur'an is not miraculous, then the whole is not. Do you sometimes dismiss God's signs because they appear small or ordinary? In what area of your life are you waiting for a 'big' miracle while missing the divine in the everyday?
?
The sarfah theory strips the Qur'an of its intrinsic power and reduces it to God's external intervention. When you read the Qur'an, do you rely on a sense of its inherent beauty and truth, or have you become numb to its words, needing a push from outside to feel its impact?
?
An-Nazzam's rationalism led him to deny certainties like tawatur and ijma. Is there an area of your faith where you are over-intellectualizing, demanding proof at every step, while neglecting the heart's submission? Will you trust in the collective wisdom of the Muslim community or insist on your own reasoning?

Refuting an-Nazzam: The Qur'an's Composition as Miracle
Refutation of an-Nazzam and counter-challenge to produce poetry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of an-Nazzam's theory and the counter-challenge using masterful poetry to demonstrate the Qur'an's inimitability.

Core Idea
The refutation proves the Qur'an's miracle lies in its composition by challenging an-Nazzam to produce even a poem equal to pre-Islamic masterpieces.

Having presented an-Nazzam's claim, the author now refutes it directly. He declares the argument false and proposes a practical challenge: if an-Nazzam thinks producing like the Qur'an is possible, let him produce a poem comparable to the famous odes of Imru' al-Qais and other Arab masters. The incapacity to do so shows the absurdity of his claim. This lesson examines that refutation and its logical force.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the Arabic passage of the refutation and its translation. Notice the author's strong dismissal: 'كلام باطل واحتجاج فاسد' (false speech and corrupt argument).
2
Identify the counter-challenge
The author challenges an-Nazzam to produce a poem comparable to those of Imru' al-Qais and other master poets. This is a practical test of his claim.
The challenge mirrors the Qur'anic challenge (tahaddi) but uses human literature.
3
Understand the logic
If an-Nazzam cannot produce even a poem of the finest human caliber, then his claim that the Qur'an is not miraculous is self-defeating. The inability shows that the Qur'an is far beyond human capacity.
4
Notice the inversion of his question
The refutation turns an-Nazzam's own question back on him: 'متى عجزت أنت؟' (when are you incapable?)—at the first word or later? This exposes his inconsistency.
The text cuts off but the argument is clear.

?

?

?

Al-Nazzam's Sarfa Theory and Its Rationale
Al-Nazzam's claim and argument for sarfa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nazzam's claim that the Quran's inimitability is due to divine prevention (al-sarfa) and his argument from the parts-whole analogy.

Core Idea
The Quran's inimitability is not inherent but due to God turning people away.

Al-Nazzam, a Mu'tazili theologian, proposed a controversial theory that the Quran is not intrinsically miraculous in its composition. According to him, any ordinary person could produce similar speech if God did not prevent them. This lesson presents his argument and its extension to other fields of knowledge, setting the stage for the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage presenting Nazzam's claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Nazzam denies the Quran's inherent inimitability and attributes it to divine prevention.
The term 'sarfa' means turning away or prevention.
2
Analyze the parts-whole argument
Nazzam argues that since individual verses are not considered miracles, the whole Quran cannot be miraculous. Understand this reasoning and its implications.
3
Examine the extension to tawatur and ijma
Nazzam applies the same logic to mutawatir reports and consensus, claiming they are not immune to error. Identify the analogy he draws.
This is a radical claim that undermines traditional epistemology.
4
Understand the beard analogy for sarfa
Nazzam illustrates sarfa with a prophet seizing his beard and challenging others. Recognize that the miracle lies in God's prevention, not in the act itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعم النظام أن (القرآن) ليس بمعجوز النظم وأن كل معجم متبلد يقدر أن يأتي بمثل نظمه العجيب ووصفه البديع، واحتجاجه لذلك أنه متى يعجز الإنسان أعند قوله: الحمد لله أم عند قوله: رب العالمين أم عند قوله: الرحمن الرحيم، وادعاء العجز عند كل من ذلك باطل لما أن ما دون سورة ليس بمعجز عندكم، ثم إذا لم يكن أفراد الآيات معجوزاً عنها، فكذا المجموع منها إذ حكم الجمل لا يخالف حكم الأفراد، ولهذا زعم أن المتواتر من الأخبار محتمل للكذب لأن خبر كل فرد من أهل التواتر محتمل للكذب فكذا عند الاجتماع، وأن الخطأ على الإجماع جائز لجوازه على كل فرد من المجتمعين، وإنما الإعجاز حصل بالصرفة ومعناها: أنه تعالى صرف همتهم بلطفه عن الاشتغال بمعارضته أنه ممكن، ومثل هذا مع معجز لما فيه من نقض العادة حتى إن من جعل دليل رسالته أن يقبض على لحية وهم لا يقبضون على ذلك، ثم إنه قبض على لحيته وتحدى الناس إلى ذلك فلم يقبض واحد منهم على لحيته كان ذلك معجزاً لما فيه من صرف هممهم عن الأمر الممكن لا أن ذلك ليس في مقدور في نفسه.
هذا منه كلام باطل واحتجاج فاسد، فإن أقرب ما يجاب عنه أن يقال: ائت بقصيدة مثل قصيدة امرئ القيس: قفا نبك، أو مثل قصيدته: ألا أنعم صباحًا أيها الطلل البالي، أو مثل قصيدته خليلي مرا بي على أم جندب أو مثل قصيدته: شمالك شرق بعدما كان أقصر، وغيرها من القصائد له ولغيره من فحول الشعراء كالنابغة وطرفه وزهير والأعشى ليظهر عند عجزه عن ذلك أنه مكابر) فيما يقول، ويقال له : متى عجزت أنت أعند التكلم بالكلمة الأولى أو عند التكلم بالكلمة أهل عصرك كصريع الغواني ومروان بن أبي حفصة وأبي نواس وسلم الخاسر وأشجع السلمى وغيرهم في نهاية الجودة والبلاغة ورشاقة النظم وجزالة الألفاظ وإحكام الصنعة؟ ألأنك مع القدرة على مثل تلك القصائد البليغة الفصيحة المحكمة اخترت لنفسك الأدون والأرذل والأبشع؟ أم لأنك عجزت عن ذلك، ولم ينقد لطبعك البارد الثقيل مثل تلك القصائد؟ ولا يخفى على أحد أن ذلك كان لعجزه عن اختياره، وكذا ما ذكر يؤدي إلى ارتفاع التفاضل بين الشعراء والخطباء والبلغاء والفصحاء وانعدام الإفحام، وحيث كان التفاضل بين هؤلاء معلومًا وانقسام الناس إلى الشعراء المعلقين والمتبلدين المفحمين أمرًا ظاهرا دل أن ذلك كلام باطل يعرف بطلانه ببديهة العقل.
وما ذكر من الحجة يوجب بطلان ما عرف ثبوته بطريق الضرورة، وهو ما ذكرنا من التفاضل بين النظم، وانقسام الناس إلى الشعراء وأهل الإفحام، وكل استدلال عرف بطلانه ضرورة كان باطلاً، لما أن الخطأ على الاستدلال جائز الضروري ممتنع ، فكان عند التعارض إضافة الخطأ إلى ما كان الخطأ فيه جائز أولي من إضافته إلى ما كان الخطأ فيه ممتنعا.
And what al-Nazzam claims: that the Quran is not inimitable in composition and that every dull ordinary person is able to produce something like its wondrous composition and magnificent description, and his argument for that is: when does a person become incapable? At his saying 'Al-hamdu lillah' or at his saying 'Rabb al-‘alamin' or at his saying 'Al-rahman al-rahim'? The claim of incapacity at each of these is false because what is less than a surah is not inimitable according to you, and if the individual verses are not inimitable, then the whole likewise, since the ruling of the whole does not differ from the ruling of the parts. And for this reason he claimed that the mutawatir reports are susceptible to falsehood because the report of each individual of the tawatur is susceptible to falsehood, so likewise when gathered together; and that error in consensus is possible because it is possible for each individual of the assembled. Rather, inimitability occurs through al-sarfa, its meaning: that the Most High turned their ambitions away by His lateness from engaging in opposing it, that it is possible. And such a thing is a miracle because it involves breaking the custom, such that if one made the proof of his message that he seize his beard while they do not seize it, then he seized his beard and challenged people to do that, and not one of them seized his beard, that would be a miracle due to the turning of their ambitions away from a possible matter, not that that is not possible in itself. This is from him false speech and corrupt argumentation, for the nearest response to him is to say: Produce a qasida like the qasida of Imru' al-Qais: 'Qifa nabki', or like his qasida: 'Ala an‘im sabaahan ayyuha al-talal al-bali', or like his qasida: 'Khalili murra bi bi ‘ala ummi jundub', or like his qasida: 'Shimaluka sharqun ba‘da ma kana aqsaru', and other qasidas of his and of other master poets like al-Nabigha, Tarafa, Zuhayr, and al-A‘sha, so that your incapacity for that may appear, that you are obstinate in what you say. And it is said to him: When did you become incapable? At the utterance of the first word or at the utterance of the word? The people of your time, like Sari‘ al-Ghawani, Marwan ibn Abi Hafsa, Abu Nuwas, Salm al-Khasir, Ashja‘ al-Sulami and others, at the utmost quality and eloquence, grace of composition, grandeur of words, and perfection of craft. Is it that you, being capable of such eloquent, eloquent, well-constructed qasidas, chose for yourself the lower, vilest, ugliest? Or because you were incapable of that, and your cold, heavy disposition did not yield such qasidas? It is not hidden from anyone that that was due to his incapacity to choose. Likewise what he mentioned leads to the elimination of differentiation among poets, orators, eloquent speakers and rhetoricians, and the absence of total bafflement. And since differentiation among these is known, and the division of people into poets who are excellent and dullards who are baffled is apparent, it indicates that that speech is false, the falsehood of which is known by the intuition of the intellect. And what he mentioned of the argument necessitates the falsity of what is known to be established by necessary knowledge, which is what we mentioned regarding differentiation in composition and the division of people into poets and those of bafflement. And every argument the falsehood of which is known necessarily is false, because error in argument is possible, while in necessary knowledge it is impossible, so when there is contradiction, attributing error to what is possible in error is more appropriate than attributing it to what error is impossible in.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (radd) of al-Nazzam's theory of sarfa regarding the inimitability (i‘jaz) of the Quran. The author first presents al-Nazzam's claim: the Quran is not inherently inimitable in its composition; rather, any ordinary person could produce something like it. Al-Nazzam argues that since individual verses are not considered inimitable (according to the opponents, because a single verse is not a miracle), the whole collection cannot be inimitable either, because the ruling of the whole follows the ruling of the parts. He extends this logic to mutawatir reports (consensus) and concludes that inimitability is achieved only by sarfa—God turning people's ambitions away from attempting to imitate it. He gives the analogy of a prophet who seizes his beard and challenges others to do the same, and no one does; this is a miracle due to divine prevention, not because the act itself is impossible.

The author then categorically states this is false and corrupt argumentation. The immediate response is to challenge al-Nazzam: produce a qasida (ode) comparable to those of Imru' al-Qais or other master poets. If he cannot, his falsehood is exposed. The author then questions whether al-Nazzam's own incapacity is due to inability or choice, sarcastically suggesting that if he were capable, he would have produced such poetry, but his cold disposition prevents it. This leads to the conclusion that the known difference in eloquence among people (poets vs. ordinary baffled individuals) is intuitive and cannot be denied. Any argument that contradicts such a necessary truth is itself false, because error is possible in reasoning but impossible in intuitive knowledge. Thus, when the two conflict, error must be attributed to the reasoning, not to the necessary knowledge.

Key Terms
النظام (al-Nazzam)
الصرفة (al-sarfa)
الإعجاز (al-i'jaz)
التواتر (al-tawatur)
الإجماع (al-ijma')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe the Quran's inimitability is solely due to divine prevention, not its linguistic quality.
Correction: This passage presents Nazzam's view; the mainstream position is that the Quran's composition itself is miraculous.

Checkpoint
What analogy does al-Nazzam use to explain sarfa?
A) A prophet seizing his beard
B) A poet composing a qasida
C) A river flowing
D) A fire burning

Key Takeaway
Al-Nazzam argues that the Quran's inimitability is not intrinsic but due to God's prevention (sarfa), using a parts-whole analogy and extending it to tawatur and ijma.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Quran's inimitability is merely God turning people away, does that weaken your personal conviction that the Quran is the ultimate miracle? Upon what do you truly base your certainty that it is from Allah?
?
Al-Nazzam's argument uses a logical fallacy to undermine not only the Quran but also tawatur and ijma. Have you ever accepted a flawed argument that eroded your trust in core Islamic teachings? What will you do to guard your faith against such reasoning?
?
The beard analogy reduces a miracle to a kind of divine trick. Does this make you uncomfortable? How does your understanding of miracles affect your daily reliance on Allah's power versus your own abilities?

Nasafi's Refutation: The Challenge of Poetry and Necessary Knowledge
Nasafi's refutation of Nazzam's sarfa theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's refutation of al-Nazzam's sarfa theory through the challenge to produce comparable poetry and the argument from necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
The Quran's inimitability is proven by the impossibility of producing similar speech, not by divine prevention.

Having presented al-Nazzam's theory, the author now refutes it forcefully. He begins by challenging al-Nazzam to produce even a single line of poetry comparable to master poets, exposing his incapacity. He then argues that the observable difference in eloquence between people is known by necessity, and any rational argument contradicting such necessary knowledge is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Start from 'هذا منه كلام باطل' and read the entire refutation. Notice how the author directly challenges al-Nazzam.
2
Analyze the challenge to produce poetry
The author dares al-Nazzam to produce a qasida like those of Imru' al-Qais or other master poets. Understand why this is a powerful rebuttal.
This challenge appeals to the known inability of even skilled poets to match the Quran.
3
Understand the argument from differentiation
The author points out that people are clearly divided into eloquent poets and ordinary baffled speakers. If Nazzam's theory were true, this differentiation would vanish.
4
Grasp the argument from necessary knowledge
The author concludes that the difference in eloquence is known by necessity (darura), and any argument against it must be false because error in necessary knowledge is impossible.
This is a key epistemological principle in kalam.

وما يزعم النظام أن (القرآن) ليس بمعجوز النظم وأن كل معجم متبلد يقدر أن يأتي بمثل نظمه العجيب ووصفه البديع، واحتجاجه لذلك أنه متى يعجز الإنسان أعند قوله: الحمد لله أم عند قوله: رب العالمين أم عند قوله: الرحمن الرحيم، وادعاء العجز عند كل من ذلك باطل لما أن ما دون سورة ليس بمعجز عندكم، ثم إذا لم يكن أفراد الآيات معجوزاً عنها، فكذا المجموع منها إذ حكم الجمل لا يخالف حكم الأفراد، ولهذا زعم أن المتواتر من الأخبار محتمل للكذب لأن خبر كل فرد من أهل التواتر محتمل للكذب فكذا عند الاجتماع، وأن الخطأ على الإجماع جائز لجوازه على كل فرد من المجتمعين، وإنما الإعجاز حصل بالصرفة ومعناها: أنه تعالى صرف همتهم بلطفه عن الاشتغال بمعارضته أنه ممكن، ومثل هذا مع معجز لما فيه من نقض العادة حتى إن من جعل دليل رسالته أن يقبض على لحية وهم لا يقبضون على ذلك، ثم إنه قبض على لحيته وتحدى الناس إلى ذلك فلم يقبض واحد منهم على لحيته كان ذلك معجزاً لما فيه من صرف هممهم عن الأمر الممكن لا أن ذلك ليس في مقدور في نفسه.

وما ذكر من الحجة يوجب بطلان ما عرف ثبوته بطريق الضرورة، وهو ما ذكرنا من التفاضل بين النظم، وانقسام الناس إلى الشعراء وأهل الإفحام، وكل استدلال عرف بطلانه ضرورة كان باطلاً، لما أن الخطأ على الاستدلال جائز الضروري ممتنع ، فكان عند التعارض إضافة الخطأ إلى ما كان الخطأ فيه جائز أولي من إضافته إلى ما كان الخطأ فيه ممتنعا.

The Objection of Prophetic Eloquence and Its Refutation
Author refutes the objection that the Prophet's eloquence explains the Quran's inimitability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Prophet's personal eloquence does not undermine the miraculous nature of the Quran's inimitability.

Core Idea
Human eloquence can only surpass others by a few lines; the Quran surpasses all by an entire book.

Some might argue that the Prophet's own superior eloquence explains why the Arabs could not produce anything like the Quran. In this passage, the author refutes this by showing that even the most eloquent humans only differ from others in a few lines of poetry or prose, not in an entire volume. The Quran's unique inimitability stands as a clear divine miracle.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the claim that the Prophet's eloquence caused the Arabs' inability to produce the like of the Quran.
The objectors use the Prophet's own statement about being the most eloquent.
2
Understand the refutation
The author explains that human eloquence is limited to small differences—only a line or two—not a whole book.
Even the best poets and orators only surpass others in a few verses.
3
Apply the logic to the Prophet's speech
The Prophet's own speech, though eloquent, is imitable in general except for a few phrases.
Thus, the Quran's inimitability is not due to his personal skill.
4
Conclude the argument
The Quran's unique inimitability is intentionally made a divine sign and a sufficient miracle.
It is not a result of human ability but a clear miracle from Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: نعم، عجزوا عن ذلك لأنه كان أفصحهم لسانًا وأجودهم بيانًا على ما روى عنه عليه السلام أنه قال: "أنا أفصح العرب"، وذلك لا يدل على أن الشعراء في قصيدة له غراء. قيل لهم: هذا جهل منكم بمبلغ الفصاحة البشرية وثبوت التفاضل فيها، فإن الفصيح من الشعراء والبليغ من الكتاب والخطباء إنما يباين غيره في بيت أو بيتين من قلائد أشعاره وأبيات قصائده، وكذا الخطيب والكاتب، فأما أن يأتي بكلام في مقدار دفتر لا يتمكن النحارير العلماء بذلك اللسان من أن يأتوا بمثل سورة قصيرة منها، فليس ذلك بموجود في الطباع، ولا بثابت في المتعارف من العادات. وكذا ما وراء القرآن من كلامه عليه السلام وإن كان فصيحا فهو مقدور غيره في الجملة، ولن يباين به غيره إلا في ألفاظ قليلة أوتي فيها جوامع الكلم. على أن إعطاء مثل ما تدعون له أنتم من الفصاحة تعالى أنه يجعل ذلك علامة لنبوته ودلالة لرسالته دلالة واضحة ومعجزة كافية.
For if they say: 'Yes, they were incapable of that because he was the most eloquent of them in tongue and the best of them in expression, according to what was narrated from him, peace be upon him, that he said: "I am the most eloquent of the Arabs," and that does not indicate that the poets [could produce] a splendid poem of his.' It is said to them: 'This is ignorance on your part of the extent of human eloquence and the establishment of differentiation in it. For the eloquent among poets and the rhetorician among writers and orators only surpasses others in one or two verses of the choicest of his poems and the lines of his odes, and similarly the orator and the writer. But as for producing speech of the size of a volume that the skilled scholars of that tongue are unable to produce the like of a short sura from it, that is not found in natural dispositions, nor is it established in customary habits. And likewise, what is beyond the Qur'an of his speech, peace be upon him, even if it is eloquent, it is within the power of others in general, and he will not surpass others by it except in a few words in which he was given the comprehensive gift of speech. Moreover, granting the like of what you claim for him of eloquence—exalted is He—that He makes that a sign for his prophethood and an evidence for his message, a clear evidence and a sufficient miracle.'
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation regarding the inimitability of the Qur'an. The objectors claim that the Prophet's own superior eloquence explains why the Arabs were unable to produce something like the Qur'an; they were simply outclassed by his natural talent. The author refutes this by arguing that even the most eloquent human beings only differ from others in a few lines of poetry or prose, not in an entire book. No matter how skilled, no human can produce a short sura like those of the Qur'an, as this is beyond natural human capacity and custom. The Prophet's own speech, though eloquent, is still imitable in general, except for a few phrases. Therefore, the Qur'an's unique eloquence is intentionally placed by God as a clear miracle, not attributable to human skill.

Key Terms
إعجاز
فصاحة
معجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet's personal eloquence diminishes the miracle of the Quran's inimitability.
Correction: The author shows that even the highest human eloquence cannot produce a whole book like the Quran, so the miracle stands.

Checkpoint

A) Because the Prophet was not actually the most eloquent.

C) Because the Arabs did not try to imitate the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's inimitability is not due to the Prophet's personal skill but is a divine miracle beyond all human capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you pause to marvel at its eloquence, or do you treat it as ordinary words? If it truly is beyond human capacity, why do your eyes often skim without awe? What will you change in your next recitation to let the weight of its miracle settle in your heart?
?
The passage states that even the most eloquent human can only surpass others in a few lines. Consider the Quran's challenge to produce a single sura like it. Have you ever entertained the thought that you could write something comparable? What does this lesson expose about your pride or underestimation of divine speech? Repent and commit to venerating the Quran as the unmatched word of Allah.
?
You have read that the Prophet's own speech, though eloquent, is imitable in general. Yet the Quran remains unique. Does this truth move you to gratitude that Allah preserved a miracle for all time? Or do you find yourself more impressed by human achievements? How can you cultivate a heart that trembles at the Quran's uniqueness today?

Beyond Eloquence: Prophecy and Legal Rulings
Author enumerates three additional miraculous aspects of the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three additional miraculous aspects of the Quran mentioned by Imam Nasafi beyond its eloquence.

Core Idea
The Quran's miracles include prophecy and comprehensive legal guidance from the All-Knowing.

Imam Nasafi has already explained the miracle of the Quran's eloquence. In this passage, he briefly points to three more dimensions of its inimitability: its accurate accounts of past nations, its predictions of future events, and its comprehensive legal rulings covering all human affairs until the end of time. He notes that such knowledge can only come from One who knows the unseen.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style and the enumeration.
2
Identify the three aspects
The passage lists three additional miraculous aspects. Find each one and notice how the author signals them with 'وجه آخر' (another aspect).
3
Reflect on the third aspect
The third aspect—comprehensive legal rulings—is argued to be impossible except from One who knows the unseen. Ask yourself why this is a powerful argument for the Quran's divine origin.
This connects to the broader theme of i'jaz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبوجه آخر من وجوه دلالات الكتاب ما تضمن من الأخبار في الأمم الماضية، ووجه آخر ما فيه من الإخبار عما يكون في المستقبل، وقد بينا الوجهين. ووجه آخر ما أودع فيه من أصول أحكام النوازل التي تقع إلى قيام الساعة ما بالناس إلى معرفته حاجة، ولن يتصور ذلك إلا من عالم بالغيوب يعلم جميع ما.

And another aspect is what is deposited in it of fundamental rulings of incidents that will occur until the establishment of the Hour, which people need to know, and that cannot be conceived except from one who knows the unseen and knows all that.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage briefly enumerates three additional miraculous aspects of the Qur'an beyond its eloquence: (1) its accurate reports about past nations, (2) its predictions about future events, and (3) its inclusion of fundamental rulings for all incidents until the Day of Judgment. The author notes that these two aspects (past and future) have already been explained, and that the third aspect—the comprehensive legal rulings—is only possible from One who knows the unseen. The passage is a concise summary of other dimensions of i'jaz.

The Miraculous Eloquence of the Quran: Refuting Deniers
Refutes those who deny the Quran's miraculous eloquence and explains human limits in eloquence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quran's eloquence is considered a miracle beyond human capacity, based on the limitation of human eloquence demonstrated by even the most gifted poets and orators.

Core Idea
Human eloquence has limits; the Quran's perfect speech surpasses all.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi refutes those who deny the miraculous nature of the Quran's eloquence. He points out that even the most eloquent poets and orators can only excel in a line or two, but the Quran presents a whole book that no expert can imitate. This demonstrates that the Quran's eloquence is beyond human nature and a clear sign of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the argument structure: the author first criticizes the deniers, then explains human limitation in eloquence, then applies it to the Quran and the Prophet's speech.
Pay attention to the contrast between ordinary eloquence and the Quran's comprehensive inimitability.
2
Identify the key contrast
The author contrasts the limited excellence of human poets and orators (only a few lines) with the Quran's ability to produce an entire book that no one can match even in a short chapter. This contrast is the core of the argument.
The word 'فإن الفصيح من الشعراء... إنما يباين غيره في بيت أو بيتين' highlights the limitation.
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that the Quran's eloquence is a clear miracle and a sign of prophethood. Note that even the Prophet's speech, though eloquent, is not miraculous except in a few words of comprehensive wisdom. The Quran alone is beyond human capacity.
This reinforces that the miracle is specific to the Quran, not the Prophet's own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشعراء في قصيدة له غراء. قيل لهم : هذا جهل منكم بمبلغ الفصاحة البشرية وثبوت التفاضل فيها، فإن الفصيح من الشعراء والبليغ من الكتاب والخطباء إنما يباين غيره في بيت أو بيتين من قلائد أشعاره وأبيات قصائده، وكذا الخطيب والكاتب، فأما أن يأتي بكلام في مقدار في دفتر لا يتمكن النحارير العلماء بذلك اللسان من أن يأتوا بمثل سورة قصيرة منها، فليس ذلك بموجود في الطباع، ولا بثابت في المتعارف من العادات وكذا ما وراء القرآن من كلامه عليه السلام وإن كان فصيحا فهو مقدور غيره في الجملة، ولن يباين به غيره الا في ألفاظ قليلة أوتى فيها جوامع الكلم على أن إعطاء مثل ما تدعون له أنتم من الفصاحة تعالى أنه يجعل ذلك علامة لنبوته ودلالة لرسالته دلالة واضحة ومعجزة كافية، والله الهادي إلى الرشاد.
The poets in a brilliant poem of his. It was said to them: This is ignorance on your part of the extent of human eloquence and the establishment of gradation in it. For the eloquent among poets and the articulate among writers and orators only surpass others in one or two verses from the gems of their poems and the lines of their odes, and likewise the orator and writer. But to produce speech of the length of a booklet that the expert scholars of that language cannot produce the like of a short sura from it—that is not found in human nature, nor established in customary habits. Likewise, the speech of the Prophet (peace be upon him) even if eloquent is generally within the capacity of others, and he only surpasses others in a few words in which he was given comprehensive speech, even though granting the eloquence that you claim for him—Exalted be He—makes it a sign of his prophethood and a clear proof of his message and a sufficient miracle. And Allah guides to the right path.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who deny the miraculous eloquence of the Quran. The author explains that even the most eloquent poets and orators only excel in a line or two, but the Quran presents a whole book that no expert can imitate even in a short sura. This is beyond human nature and custom. Even the Prophet's own speech, though eloquent, is within human capacity except in few words of comprehensive wisdom. Thus, the Quran's eloquence is a clear miracle and sign of prophethood.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's eloquence is a miracle because it surpasses the natural limits of human linguistic ability, as even the most eloquent humans can only excel in a few lines, whereas the Quran remains inimitable.

The Quran's Miracles: Past, Future, and Legal Rulings
Introduces three miraculous aspects of the Quran: past, future, and legal rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Quran contains miraculous aspects beyond poetry, including accurate knowledge of the past, prophecies of the future, and comprehensive legal rulings that could only come from an all-knowing Being.

Core Idea
The Quran's divine origin is shown by its knowledge of past, future, and perfect laws.

The author, Imam al-Nasafi, continues to unfold the multifaceted miraculous nature of the Quran. After discussing its linguistic inimitability, he now points to other dimensions: the Quran contains precise accounts of bygone nations, predictions of future events, and a complete set of fundamental rulings for all occurrences until the end of time. This lesson introduces these three categories and establishes that the third category—the legal rulings—demands a source that knows all unseen and all wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through. Then read the English translation. Notice how the author lists three aspects of dalaalat (indications) of the Quran.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage mentions three aspects: news of past nations, news of future events, and fundamental rulings for all events. Underline or note each one.
The author says he has already explained the first two aspects elsewhere, focusing here on the third.
3
Reflect on the third category
The third category—legal rulings—requires knowledge of all future events and their wisdom. Ask yourself: why is this impossible for a human?
This sets up the miraculous nature of the rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبوجه آخر من وجوه دلالات الكتاب ما تضمن من الأخبار في الأمم الماضية، ووجه آخر ما فيه من الإخبار عما يكون في المستقبل، وقد بينا الوجهين. ووجه آخر ما أودع فيه من أصول أحكام النوازل التي تقع إلى قيام الساعة ما بالناس إلى معرفته حاجة، ولن يتصور ذلك إلا من عالم بالغيوب يعلم جميع ما يكون، ويعلم وجه الحكمة والصواب في كل ذلك، ليودع ذلك كله الكتاب، فكان فيه الإعجاز من أوجه ثلاثة: أحدها العلم بما يكون في المستقبل إلى قيام الساعة، وهو علم الغيب لا يعلمه إلا الله تعالى. والثاني: العلم بوجه الحكمة والمصلحة في كل ذلك، وليس في طباع البشر أن يعلم رجل أمي لم ينفق عمره في إحراز العلوم واكتساب الآداب والحكم وجهة الحكمة والصواب والمصلحة في حوادث أسبوع واحد لإنسان واحد من جنس واحد فضلاً عن جميع أجناس الحوادث الواقعة في العبادات، وأنواع المعاملات والمواريث والمعاشرات لجميع العالم إلى آباد الدهر، فعلم أن ذلك ورد من عند علام الغيوب الذي لا يعزب عنه شيء ولا يخفى عليه خافية. والثالث: أنه ليس في مقدور البشر أن يودع في واحد منهم في مقدار دفتر من الكتاب ما يربو من العلوم عند التفتيش والإيضاح والبسط على ألف دفتر وزيادة، فدل أن ذلك بالقادر الذي لا يصعب عليه أمر ولا يعجزه شيء، والله الموفق.
And another aspect of the indications of the Book is what it contains of news about past nations, and another aspect is what it contains of information about what will happen in the future, and we have explained both aspects. And another aspect is what is deposited in it of the fundamental rulings of the events that occur until the Day of Resurrection, which people need to know; and that cannot be conceived except from a Knower of the Unseen who knows all that will be, and knows the aspect of wisdom and correctness in all of that, so that He deposits all of that in the Book. So in it there is miraculousness from three aspects: First, the knowledge of what will happen in the future until the Day of Resurrection, which is knowledge of the Unseen known only to Allah Almighty. Second, the knowledge of the aspect of wisdom and benefit in all of that, and it is not in human nature that an illiterate man who has not spent his life acquiring sciences and learning etiquette and wisdom should know the aspect of wisdom, correctness, and benefit in the events of one week for one person of one species, let alone all kinds of events occurring in acts of worship, types of transactions, inheritances, and social dealings for the entire world until the end of time. So it is known that this came from the Knower of the Unseen from whom nothing is absent and no hidden thing is concealed. Third, it is not within human capacity to deposit in one of them, in the size of a booklet of the Book, sciences that when investigated, clarified, and expanded amount to a thousand booklets and more. So it proves that this is from the Powerful One for whom no matter is difficult and nothing incapacitates Him. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage enumerates other aspects of the Quran's miraculous nature. First, it contains news of past nations. Second, it contains prophecies of the future. Third, and most detailed, it contains the fundamental rulings for all events until the Day of Resurrection, which requires knowledge of the Unseen and perfect wisdom—something impossible for an unlettered man. The author then breaks down the miraculousness of these rulings into three specific aspects: (1) knowledge of future events until the end of time, which is only known to Allah; (2) knowledge of the wisdom and benefit in all those rulings, which no human could possess; (3) the vast amount of knowledge condensed in a small book, which would fill thousands of volumes. Each aspect proves the divine origin of the Quran.

Key Terms
دلالات الكتاب
أخبار الماضي
أخبار المستقبل
أحكام النوازل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) News of past nations
B) Predictions of future events
C) Scientific facts about the universe
D) Fundamental rulings for all events

Key Takeaway
The Quran's miraculous nature extends beyond eloquence to include accurate historical accounts, future prophecies, and a comprehensive legal system that only an all-knowing God could provide.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage argues that only an all-knowing God could compile such a book. If you truly believe the Quran is from Allah, why do you sometimes hesitate to follow its commands completely? What fear holds you back?
?
The Quran contains accurate news of the past and future. How does this certainty affect your daily life? Do you live with the awareness that the Quran's prophecies are true, or do you treat them as distant history? What would change if you fully internalized that the Quran's every word is from the Knower of the Unseen?

Three Miraculous Aspects of the Quran's Legal Rulings
Enumerates three miraculous aspects of the Quran's legal rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three specific ways in which the Quran's legal rulings exemplify divine origin: knowledge of the future, knowledge of wisdom and benefit, and the vast knowledge condensed in its small volume.

Core Idea
The Quran's laws are miraculous because they require knowledge of the future, perfect wisdom, and immense conciseness.

Having introduced the legal rulings as a miraculous aspect, Imam al-Nasafi now breaks down exactly why these rulings are beyond human capacity. He presents three distinct points: (1) they contain knowledge of all future events until the end of time, (2) they reflect perfect wisdom and benefit in every ruling, and (3) the immense amount of knowledge packed into a small book. Each point independently proves that the Quran is from Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three aspects
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. The passage begins with 'So in it there is miraculousness from three aspects...' and then lists them. Underline each aspect.
2
Analyze the first aspect: knowledge of the future
The first aspect is knowledge of future events until the Day of Resurrection, which is exclusive to Allah. Think about why this is miraculous for an unlettered prophet.
This connects to the earlier point about prophecies.
3
Analyze the second aspect: knowledge of wisdom and benefit
The author argues that even knowing the wisdom behind one week's events for one person is impossible for a human, let alone all rulings for all people until eternity. Savor the rhetorical escalation from one week to all time.
The author uses hyperbole to emphasize impossibility.
4
Analyze the third aspect: condensation of knowledge
The Quran, in the size of a small booklet, contains knowledge that would fill thousands of volumes. Consider the miracle of conciseness with depth.

Source Text

The Mu'tazila Denial of Saintly Miracles
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila argument against saintly miracles with two reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument for the impossibility of saintly miracles and the two reasons they give.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila deny saintly miracles to protect the distinctiveness of prophetic miracles.

The Mu'tazila argued that saintly miracles would obscure the very purpose of prophetic miracles. To understand their reasoning, we must examine two key points: the danger of confusion and the absence of need. This lesson presents their argument as found in Nasafi's text.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila claim
Read the passage where Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila position. Notice the logical structure: first the conclusion, then two reasons.
The Mu'tazila state their conclusion clearly: saintly miracles are impossible.
2
Identify the first reason
The first reason is that saintly miracles would be confused with prophetic miracles, making it impossible to know a true prophet.
This is a logical argument: if both types of miracles occur, one cannot distinguish them.
3
Identify the second reason
The second reason is that breaking natural custom (naqḍ al-ʿāda) serves the purpose of identifying prophets, a need that does not exist for saints.
The Mu'tazila argue that saintly miracles would be purposeless.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider why the Mu'tazila consider the impossibility absolute. How would the Sunni response challenge this?
This prepares you for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني: الكلام في إثبات كرامات الأولياء. ومن هذا القبيل كرامات الأولياء أن إظهارها عليهم هل هو في حيز الممكنات أم هو ممتنع؟ فزعمت المعتزلة أنه ممتنع لأنه لو جاز لالتبست المعجزة بالكرامة، ولم يبق إلى الوصول إلى العلم بكون النبي نبيًا طريق، وهذا ممتنع، ولأن نقض العادة لفائدة الوصول إلى معرفة النبي والتمييز بينه وبين المتنبي وبالناس إلى ذلك حاجة ماسة، فأما الحاجة إلى معرفة الولي من غير الولي فمنعدمة. وأهل الحق يقولون بثبوتها لورود أخبار كثيرة وحكايات مستفيضة منها استفاضة الخبر عن صاحب سليمان في إتيانه بعرش بلقيس قبل ارتداد الطرف. ومنها رؤية عمر على المنبر بالمدينة جيشه بنهاوند حتى قال: يا سارية الجبل وسمع سارية ذلك الصوت على مسافة ذهاب قريب من خمسمائة فرسخ حتى صعد الجبل وأخرج منه الكمين على البدء، وكان ذلك سبب الفتح. وما روى أن خالدًا شرب السم فلم يضره، وما روى من خبر عمر في أمر نهر الجبل.
The second chapter: Discussion on the affirmation of the miracles of saints. And of this kind are the miracles of saints: is their manifestation upon them within the realm of possibilities or is it impossible? The Mu'tazila claimed that it is impossible, because if it were possible, the prophetic miracle (al-muʿjiza) would be confounded with the saintly miracle (al-karāma), and no way would remain to attain knowledge that a prophet is a prophet, and this is impossible. And because breaking the custom (naqḍ al-ʿāda) has the benefit of attaining knowledge of the prophet and distinguishing between him and the false prophet, and people have an urgent need for that. As for the need to know the saint (walī) from the non-saint, it is absent. And the people of truth (Ahl al-Ḥaqq) affirm their occurrence due to many reports and widespread stories that have come: among them the widespread report about the companion of Solomon in bringing the throne of Bilqis before the blink of an eye. And among them is the vision of ʿUmar on the pulpit in Medina of his army in Nahawand until he said, 'O Sāriya, the mountain!' and Sāriya heard that voice at a distance of nearly five hundred parasangs, so he ascended the mountain and brought out the ambush from it at the start, and that was the cause of the conquest. And what was narrated that Khālid drank poison and it did not harm him, and what was narrated of the report of ʿUmar regarding the matter of the river of the mountain.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal argument on the possibility of saintly miracles (karāmāt al-awliyāʾ). The author first frames the question: are such miracles possible or impossible? Then presents the Muʿtazila position: they deem it impossible for two reasons. First, if saintly miracles were possible, they would be confused with prophetic miracles (muʿjizāt), thereby eliminating the means to know a prophet's truthfulness. Second, the breaking of natural custom (naqḍ al-ʿāda) serves the purpose of identifying prophets and distinguishing them from false claimants, a need the people urgently have. There is no similar need to distinguish a saint from a non-saint, so saintly miracles lack purpose. The author then gives the Sunni (Ahl al-Ḥaqq) response: they affirm saintly miracles based on abundant transmitted reports. He provides four examples: (1) the companion of Solomon (Āṣaf ibn Barkhiyā) bringing the throne of the Queen of Sheba in an instant; (2) ʿUmar ibn al-Khaṭṭāb seeing his army in Nahawand from the pulpit in Medina and calling out to Sāriya, who heard him from a great distance and acted accordingly, leading to victory; (3) Khālid ibn al-Walīd drinking poison without harm; (4) another report of ʿUmar concerning a river from a mountain. These examples serve as evidence that such events occur and are not impossible.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
المعجزة
الكرامة
نقض العادة
الولي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Saintly miracles are impossible because they are supernatural and thus false.
Correction: The Mu'tazila do not deny supernatural events in principle; they deny saintly miracles specifically because they would undermine the evidentiary function of prophetic miracles.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason the Mu'tazila give for denying saintly miracles?
A) Saintly miracles would be confused with prophetic miracles, making prophethood unknowable.
B) Saintly miracles are impossible because they contradict the laws of nature.
C) There is no evidence for saintly miracles in the Quran.
D) Saintly miracles would diminish the status of prophets.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that saintly miracles are impossible because they would confuse prophethood and serve no necessary purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly wished for a miraculous sign to confirm your faith, rather than trusting the clear proofs of prophethood? How does this Mu'tazila argument expose that desire as a lack of trust in what God has already provided? Will you repent of that and rely solely on the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
The Mu'tazila say that saintly miracles serve no necessary purpose. Do you sometimes seek extra spiritual experiences or 'special favours' from God to feel close to Him, rather than being content with the obligatory acts? Does that attitude reflect ingratitude for the gift of faith? What will you change today?
?
Are you ever tempted to elevate a righteous person—a teacher or a scholar—to a status that blurs the line between prophet and saint? How does this lesson remind you that only prophets have the unique role of bringing divine law? Will you guard your heart against shirk in any form?

The Sunni Affirmation of Saintly Miracles
Nasafi lists four examples of saintly miracles from Islamic tradition to support the Sunni position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni evidence for saintly miracles and the four examples given by Nasafi.

Core Idea
Sunnis affirm saintly miracles through transmitted reports of extraordinary events.

Contrary to the Mu'tazila, the Sunni tradition holds that saintly miracles are possible and have occurred. Nasafi provides four narrated examples to support this position. Let's explore each one and see how they serve as evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Sunni response
Read the passage where Nasafi presents the Sunni position. Notice the general statement that saintly miracles are affirmed due to many reports.
The Sunni response is not philosophical but based on transmitted reports.
2
Study the first example
The companion of Solomon (Asaf ibn Barkhiyā) brought the throne of Bilqis in an instant. This is a clear saintly miracle.
This event is mentioned in the Quran (Surah An-Naml).
3
Study the second example
Umar ibn al-Khattab, from the pulpit in Medina, saw his army in Nahawand and called out. Sariya heard him from a great distance.
This story is well-known in Islamic tradition.
4
Study the third and fourth examples
Khalid ibn al-Walid drank poison unharmed, and another report of Umar about a river from a mountain. These show saintly miracles.
These examples demonstrate that saintly miracles are not limited to one type.
5
Reflect on the evidence
Consider how these examples collectively argue for the possibility of saintly miracles. How do they refute the Mu'tazila?
The examples show that such events are part of Islamic history.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني: الكلام في إثبات كرامات الأولياء. ومن هذا القبيل كرامات الأولياء أن إظهارها عليهم هل هو في حيز الممكنات أم هو ممتنع؟ فزعمت المعتزلة أنه ممتنع لأنه لو جاز لالتبست المعجزة بالكرامة، ولم يبق إلى الوصول إلى العلم بكون النبي نبيًا طريق، وهذا ممتنع، ولأن نقض العادة لفائدة الوصول إلى معرفة النبي والتمييز بينه وبين المتنبي وبالناس إلى ذلك حاجة ماسة، فأما الحاجة إلى معرفة الولي من غير الولي فمنعدمة. وأهل الحق يقولون بثبوتها لورود أخبار كثيرة وحكايات مستفيضة منها استفاضة الخبر عن صاحب سليمان في إتيانه بعرش بلقيس قبل ارتداد الطرف. ومنها رؤية عمر على المنبر بالمدينة جيشه بنهاوند حتى قال: يا سارية الجبل وسمع سارية ذلك الصوت على مسافة ذهاب قريب من خمسمائة فرسخ حتى صعد الجبل وأخرج منه الكمين على البدء، وكان ذلك سبب الفتح. وما روى أن خالدًا شرب السم فلم يضره، وما روى من خبر عمر في أمر نهر الجبل.
The second chapter: Discussion on the affirmation of the miracles of saints. And of this kind are the miracles of saints: is their manifestation upon them within the realm of possibilities or is it impossible? The Mu'tazila claimed that it is impossible, because if it were possible, the prophetic miracle (al-muʿjiza) would be confounded with the saintly miracle (al-karāma), and no way would remain to attain knowledge that a prophet is a prophet, and this is impossible. ?

?

?
The Sunnis affirm these events because they are widely transmitted. In an age where you have access to countless reports, how do you determine which ones to believe with certainty? What is your standard of truth?

The Mu'tazila Denial of Karamat and Its Consequences
Refutes Mu'tazila denial and uses their deprivation as evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila denial of karamat and how their deprivation of such miracles serves as a proof of the falsehood of their doctrines.

Core Idea
The absence of karamat among the Mu'tazila is itself a sign of their corruption.

The author begins by addressing the Mu'tazila's rejection of saintly miracles (karamat). He argues that their denial stems from the ill omen of their innovation, and that the very fact that no karamah ever appears among them, despite their diligence in worship, is a clear indicator of their falsehood. Let us read the passage to see how the author uses this as a proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Mu'tazila denial
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's strong language against the Mu'tazila.
The passage is polemical but argumentative.
2
Identify the argument from deprivation
The author presents a conditional argument: if there were no other proof, their deprivation of karamat alone would suffice. Find this line and understand its logic.
He describes their outward state as a mark of humiliation.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why does the author consider this deprivation as evidence? Connect it to the Mu'tazila's corrupt beliefs.
The commentary will clarify this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنكرت المعتزلة ذلك لأنهم حرموا ذلك لشؤم بدعتهم، ولو لم يكن على بطلان مذاهبهم وفساد عقائدهم دليل سوى حرمانهم الكرامة مع جدهم واجتهادهم في العبادات وشدة توقيهم عن المعاصي والآثام خوفًا من خروجهم عن الإيمان، ثم لم يكن يظهر على أحد منهم كرامة، ولم يعاين أحد منهم ذلك في نفسه ليرجع عنه ويعود إلى الإقرار به، ولم يكن ذلك إلا لبقائهم بسبب عقائدهم الفاسدة أعداء الله تعالى مستحقين للإهانة فيرى الأكثر منهم وقد تعلوهم صفرة سمجة تنبو الأبصار عن النظر إلى طلعته، وتثقل على القلوب معاشرته وصحبته. كرامة الولي معجزة للرسول إنكاره. وما زعموا أن ظهورها على يدي الأولياء يوجب بطلان معجزات الأنبياء عليهم السلام، فكلام من يتفوه بما يخطر بباله من غير التأمل والتروي والعرض على العقل الذي هو القانون الذي بشهادته يمتاز [؟] الحق من الباطل، والصحيح من الفاسد، وذلك لأنه لو تأمل لعرف أن كل كرامة لوليّ معجزة للرسول عليه السلام فإن بظهورها يعلم أنه ولي وكونه وليًا على كونه محقا في عقيدته وهو فيها تابع لرسول مقر برسالته دليل على صحة رسالته، فكان ذلك على هذا التدريج دليلاً على صدق الرسول فيما ادعاه من الرسالة وبلغ من الدين والشريعة، فمن جعل ما هو معجزة للرسول ودلالة صدقه مبطلاً للمعجزة وسادًا لطريق الوصول إلى معرفتها فهو في غاية الجهل والغباوة.
And the Mu'tazila denied that because they were deprived of it due to the ill omen of their innovation. If there were no proof of the falsehood of their doctrines and the corruption of their beliefs other than their deprivation of karamah (saintly miracle) despite their seriousness and their diligence in worship and their intense guarding against sins and transgressions out of fear of leaving faith, then no karamah appeared from any of them, and none of them witnessed it in himself to recant and return to affirming it. And that was only because, due to their corrupt beliefs, they remained enemies of Allah, deserving of humiliation. So you see most of them with a sallow complexion that eyes shun from looking at, and their company and companionship weigh heavy on hearts. The karamah of the saint is a miracle (mu'jizah) for the messenger. Denial of it. And they claimed that its appearance at the hands of saints necessitates the invalidation of the miracles (mu'jizat) of the prophets, peace be upon them. This is the speech of one who utters whatever comes to his mind without reflection, deliberation, and presentation to the intellect, which is the criterion by which truth is distinguished from falsehood and sound from corrupt. That is because if he reflected, he would know that every karamah of a saint is a miracle for the messenger, peace be upon him. For by its appearance it is known that he is a saint, and his being a saint upon being truthful in his creed—and in it he is a follower of a messenger, affirming his message—is a proof of the validity of his message. So this by this gradation becomes evidence of the truthfulness of the messenger in his claim of messengership and what he conveyed of religion and law. So whoever makes what is a miracle for the messenger and a proof of his truthfulness into an invalidator of the miracle and a barrier to the way of knowing it is in the utmost ignorance and stupidity.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila who denied the occurrence of karamat (miracles of saints). The author argues that the Mu'tazila themselves are deprived of karamat due to their corrupt beliefs, which is a sign of their falsehood. He describes their outward state as a mark of humiliation. Then he addresses their objection that karamat would invalidate prophetic miracles. He explains that every karamah is actually a mu'jizah (miracle) for the prophet, because it proves the saint's following of the prophet, thus confirming the prophet's truthfulness. The argument is that the saint is known to be a saint through the karamah, and his being a saint in a true creed—which includes following a messenger—serves as a proof of that messenger's truth. Therefore, denying karamat leads to ignorance and stupidity, as one would be rejecting evidence that supports the prophetic message.

Key Terms
كرامة
معتزلة
ولي
إنكار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Karamat do not occur or are irrelevant to the truth of doctrines.
Correction: The author proves that karamat do occur, and their absence among the Mu'tazila is actually evidence of their falsehood.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the significance of the Mu'tazila not experiencing karamat?
A) It proves their doctrines are false.
B) It shows they are not sincere in worship.
C) It means karamat are not real.
D) It is due to chance.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's denial of karamat and their deprivation of them is itself a proof of the corruption of their beliefs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you strive in worship but see no spiritual fruit—like peace, closeness to Allah, or answered prayers—do you consider that you might be deprived due to some unrecognized wrong belief in your heart? Will you examine yourself today for any innovation or false assumption you hold despite your outward devotion?
?
The passage says the Mu'tazila had a sallow complexion and burdensome company. Have you ever felt a heaviness in your own presence or that of others that might be a sign of something wrong in your faith? If you find that your company weighs on hearts, what change in your beliefs or actions must you make to become a source of blessing instead?
?
Imagine you were as diligent in worship as the Mu'tazila, yet you denied a truth about Allah's saints—their karamat. If you saw no miracle in your life despite your efforts, would you be willing to admit your error and change your belief? Or would you persist, convinced you are right, even if evidence points otherwise?

Karamah as a Miracle for the Prophet
Argues that each karamah is a miracle for the prophet and refutes the objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why every karamah of a saint is actually a miracle for the prophet, and how this refutes the Mu'tazila objection.

Core Idea
A saint's karamah confirms the prophet's truthfulness, not invalidates it.

The Mu'tazila argued that karamat would invalidate prophetic miracles. The author responds with a powerful argument: every karamah is itself a miracle for the prophet, because it demonstrates the saint's following of the prophet. Let us examine this argument in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the argument
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the chain of reasoning. The author refutes the Mu'tazila claim step by step.
Pay attention to the gradation: karamah -> saint -> prophet.
2
Understand the logical chain
The author explains: a karamah shows the person is a saint; his sainthood with correct creed shows he is a follower of a prophet; this affirms the prophet's message.
This is a cumulative proof.
3
Appreciate the refutation
The Mu'tazila claim that karamat invalidate miracles is shown to be ignorance, because karamat actually support them.
Consider the author's strong language at the end.

?
Have you ever witnessed or heard of a saintly person performing something extraordinary? ?
The author says the Mu'tazila objection arises from 'one who utters whatever comes to his mind without reflection, deliberation, and presentation to the intellect.' Where in your own life do you make snap judgments about religious matters without the careful reflection this passage calls for? What habit do you need to change?
?
If every karamah is a miracle for the Prophet, then the righteous acts of saints are not just personal favors—they are divine confirmations of the religion. How does this raise your accountability when you see piety in others? Are you using these signs to strengthen your faith, or are you ignoring them?

6 min
Distinguishing Prophetic Miracles from Saintly Miracles
Distinguishes mu'jizah and karamah by their relation to claims, concealment, and infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to distinguish a prophetic miracle (mu'jizah) from a saintly miracle (karamah) based on the claimant's status, concealment, and infallibility.

Core Idea
A prophetic miracle is openly manifested and confirms a claim, while a saintly miracle is concealed and feared as possible entrapment.

In this passage, the author clarifies the key differences between the miracles of prophets and those of saints. He explains that a mu'jizah follows a claim of prophethood and is displayed openly, whereas a karamah is hidden by the saint who fears it might be a form of divine entrapment. Understanding these distinctions prevents confusion between the two types of extraordinary events.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note how the author begins by stating the distinction between mu'jizah, karamah, ma'unah, and ihana, then immediately focuses on confusing karamah with mu'jizah.
The author uses a question to introduce the need for distinction.
2
Identify the characteristics of a mu'jizah
Look for the key features: it appears after a claim of prophethood, the prophet manifests it openly, and the prophet is infallible and secure in consequence.
3
Identify the characteristics of a karamah
Find the features: the saint conceals it, fears it might be istidraj (gradual entrapment), and if he claimed sainthood it would be taken away.
Also note that the saint is not infallible like the prophet.
4
Compare the two types
Contrast the open manifestation vs. concealment, the relation to a claim, and the security of outcome. Understand why a saint cannot claim prophethood.
This comparison helps avoid confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرق بين المعجزة والكرامة والمعونة والإهانة ثم نقول : وكيف يؤدي ذلك إلى التباس الكرامة بالمعجزة، والمعجزة تظهر على أثر دعوى الرسالة، ولو ادعى الولي الرسالة لكفر من ساعته، وصار عدوا الله لا يظهر على يده نقض العادة البتة، ولو ادعى الولاية تسقط عنه الولاية عند دعواه إياها، وكذا صاحب المعجزة لا يكتم معجزته بل يظهرها، وصاحب الكرامة يجتهد في كتمانها ولا يركن في الأغلب إليها، ويخاف أنها من قبيل الاستدراج له دون الكرامة، ولو اطلع الله تعالى أحدًا من صالحي عباده على كرامة الولي لتشفع إليه الولي وطلب بغاية التضرع أن يكتم ذلك عليه ولا يفشيه خوفا من الاغترار لدى الاشتهار، وكذا صاحب المعجزة مأمون العاقبة معصوم عن التبديل والولي بخلافه. وما ذكروا أن لا فائدة منها قلنا: وإذا ثبت بما ذكرنا من الدلائل أنها ثابتة فبعد ذلك جهلكم بجهة الحكمة لا يوجب بطلانها على ما مر تقرير هذا من قبل. ثم نقول : فائدته ظهور كرامته على الله تعالى عنده، وثبوت رسالة من أمن به من الرسل بما شاهد من نقض العادة، فيصير كأحد من كان في عصر الأنبياء في حق مشاهدة المعجزة، ومعاينة نقض العادة مع أن ذلك يصير مبعثه له على الاجتهاد في العبادات والاحتراس عن السيئات إبقاء لتلك المنزلة على نفسه، وحفظاً لتلك المرتبة الشريفة والمنزلة العلية عن التبدل والزوال، وتحريضا لمن أطلعه الله عليها من الصالحين على الجد والاجتهاد ليبلغ تلك الدرجة وتناله تلك المنزلة. وقال أهل العلم بالحقائق إن ظهور الناقض للعادة ربما يكون من الفرط لواحد من عوام المسلمين، وتكون معونة له في التخلص عن محنة توجهت إليه،
The difference between mu'jizah (prophetic miracle), karamah (saintly miracle), ma'unah (divine assistance), and ihana (humiliation). Then we say: How does that lead to confusion between karamah and mu'jizah? The mu'jizah appears after a claim of prophethood. If a saint claimed prophethood, he would disbelieve instantly and become an enemy of Allah, so no breach of custom (naqd al-'adah) would ever appear at his hand. And if he claimed sainthood, sainthood would be removed from him at his claim. Likewise, the possessor of a mu'jizah does not conceal his miracle but manifests it, while the possessor of a karamah strives to conceal it and generally does not rely on it, fearing that it might be a form of istidraj (gradual entrapment) for him rather than a karamah. If Allah informs one of His righteous servants of a saint's karamah, the saint would intercede and plead earnestly that it be concealed from him and not divulged, for fear of self-conceit upon becoming famous. Similarly, the possessor of a mu'jizah is secure in consequence and infallible from alteration, while the saint is not. And what they mentioned that there is no benefit in it? We say: If it has been established by the proofs we have mentioned that it is real, then after that your ignorance of the aspect of wisdom does not necessitate its invalidity, as has been explained before. Then we say: Its benefit is that it manifests the saint's honor with Allah, and it establishes the message of the messenger in whom he believed, by what he witnessed of the breach of custom. He becomes like one of those who lived in the time of the prophets regarding witnessing the miracle and seeing the breach of custom. Moreover, that becomes a motivation for him to strive in worship and to guard against sins, in order to preserve that station for himself and to protect that noble rank and high station from change and loss, and as an encouragement for those righteous ones upon whom Allah has bestowed it to strive in order to reach that degree and attain that station. And the scholars of realities said: The appearance of the breach of custom may sometimes occur for an ordinary Muslim as a ma'unah (assistance) to save him from a calamity that has befallen him.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between four types of extraordinary events: mu'jizah (prophetic miracle), karamah (saintly miracle), ma'unah (divine assistance), and ihana (humiliation). The author explains that a mu'jizah follows a claim of prophethood and is openly manifested, while a karamah is concealed by the saint, who fears it might be istidraj (entrapment). A saint would never claim prophethood, and if he claimed sainthood, it would be taken away. The possessor of a mu'jizah is infallible, while the saint is not. The author then addresses the objection that karamat have no benefit, listing several: it shows the saint's honor with Allah, it confirms the prophet's message for the witness, it motivates the saint to greater devotion, and it encourages others to strive. Finally, he notes that a breach of custom can also be a ma'unah (assistance) for an ordinary Muslim to escape a calamity, not necessarily a karamah.

Key Terms
معجزة
كرامة
استدراج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Saints can perform miracles like prophets, and both are the same type.
Correction: A saint's karamah is distinct: it does not accompany a claim of prophethood, the saint hides it, and the saint is not infallible.

Checkpoint
Why does a saint try to conceal his karamah?
A) Because he is commanded to do so by the prophet.
B) For fear that it might be a form of istidraj (entrapment) or self-conceit.
C) Because the Mu'tazila forbid showing them.
D) Because karamat are not real.

Key Takeaway
Mu'jizah confirms prophethood and is openly shown, while karamah is hidden by the saint and does not validate any claim to prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The saint fears that his apparent miracle might actually be istidraj—a gradual entrapment. Think of a blessing or success you experienced recently. Did you thank Allah, or did you feel self-satisfied? Are you sure it was a reward and not a test drawing you away from Him? Commit today to thank Allah for every blessing and to examine your heart for pride.
?
The prophet openly manifests his miracle to guide others, even if it brings scrutiny. Is there an aspect of your faith that you hide for fear of others' opinions? Will you stand for the truth publicly, even if it costs you comfort or reputation? Decide one specific truth you will share or defend this week.
?
The saint strives to conceal his good deeds to avoid self-conceit. Reflect on a good deed you did recently: did you hope someone would notice? If so, repent and seek sincerity. What practical step can you take to ensure your next act of worship is purely for Allah alone?

Miracles
intermediate
5 min
The Benefits of Saintly Miracles and Divine Assistance
Lists benefits of karamah and defines ma'unah as divine assistance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the benefits of saintly miracles (karamat) and the concept of divine assistance (ma'unah) for ordinary Muslims.

Core Idea
Karamat have clear benefits: they affirm the saint's honor, confirm prophethood, motivate devotion, and inspire others; ma'unah aids ordinary believers in distress.

After establishing that saintly miracles are real, the author addresses the objection that they serve no purpose. He lists several benefits, from confirming the prophet's message to encouraging righteous conduct. He then introduces ma'unah, a form of divine assistance that can occur for any Muslim facing hardship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage that answers the objection
Read the Arabic and translation starting from 'وما ذكروا أن لا فائدة منها'. Understand that the author responds to critics who claim karamah has no benefit.
The author argues that ignorance of the wisdom does not invalidate the reality.
2
List the four benefits of karamah
Identify: (1) It manifests the saint's honor with Allah. (2) It confirms the message of the prophet for the witness. (3) It motivates the saint to greater worship and avoidance of sins. (4) It encourages other righteous people to strive for that rank.
Each benefit has a distinct purpose.
3
Reflect on the purpose of each benefit
Consider how each benefit strengthens faith, provides evidence, or inspires action. Notice the phrase 'as if he lived in the time of the prophets'.
4
Understand ma'unah as a distinct category
Read the final sentence: 'وقال أهل العلم بالحقائق...' that a breach of custom can also be a ma'unah for an ordinary Muslim to escape a calamity. This is different from karamah.
Ma'unah is not limited to saints; it is divine help for any believer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Karamah has no benefit and is therefore impossible.
Correction: The author lists several benefits of karamah, including confirming prophethood and motivating worship, refuting the objection.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a benefit of karamah?
A) It proves that the saint is a prophet.
B) It confirms the message of the prophet for the witness.
C) It allows the saint to perform any miracle he wishes.
D) It eliminates the need for worship.

Key Takeaway
Karamat serve multiple spiritual purposes, including strengthening faith and inspiring devotion, and ma'unah is a distinct form of divine help for ordinary people in distress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever experienced a moment of divine assistance that rescued you from a dire situation? Did that experience move you to greater devotion and gratitude, or did you take it for granted? Commit now to a specific act of thanks for that aid.
?
The lesson says that witnessing a karamah or ma'unah should motivate one to strive in worship. Is your current level of worship driven by such inspiration, or have you become complacent? What one change can you make today to seek that higher station?
?
If you were granted a karamah, would you use it to draw closer to Allah, or would you be tempted to become proud? Examine your heart: is there any pride in your worship that needs to be repented of?
